(disambiguation)">Asia Minor (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox vcard" style="width:22em"><caption class="fn org">Anatolia</caption><tbody><tr><th people">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laz_people_in_Turkey" title="Laz people in Turkey">Laz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Turkey" title="Demographics of Turkey">various others</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Anatolia</b> (from  <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#7944;&#957;&#945;&#964;&#959;&#955;&#942;</span> <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Anatol&#7703;</i></i>; <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">Anadolu</i> "east" or "[sun]rise"), also known as <b>Asia Minor</b> (<a href="/wiki/Medieval_Greek" title="Medieval Greek">Medieval</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#924;&#953;&#954;&#961;&#940; &#7944;&#963;&#943;&#945;</span> <i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Mikr&#225; As&#237;a</i></i>, "small Asia"; <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>: <i lang="tr">K&#252;&#231;&#252;k Asya</i>), <b>Asian Turkey</b>, the <b>Anatolian peninsula</b>, or the <b>Anatolian plateau</b>, is the westernmost protrusion of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, which makes up the majority of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>. The region is bounded by the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> to the north, the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a> to the south, the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Highlands" title="Armenian Highlands">Armenian Highlands</a> to the east, and the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean Sea</a> to the west. The <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Marmara" title="Sea of Marmara">Sea of Marmara</a> forms a connection between the Black and Aegean Seas through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bosphorus" title="Bosphorus">Bosphorus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> straits and separates Anatolia from <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> mainland.\n</p><p>The eastern border of Anatolia is traditionally held to be a line between the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Alexandretta" title="Gulf of Alexandretta">Gulf of Alexandretta</a> and the Black Sea, bounded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_Highland" title="Armenian Highland">Armenian Highland</a> to the east and Mesopotamia to the southeast. Thus, traditionally Anatolia is the territory that comprises approximately the western two-thirds of the Asian part of Turkey. Nowadays, Anatolia is also often considered to be synonymous with Asian Turkey, which comprises almost the entire country;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> its eastern and southeastern borders are widely taken to be <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>\'s eastern border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxfordhb_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxfordhb-6">[6]</a></sup> By some definitions, the area called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_highlands" title="Armenian highlands">Armenian highlands</a> lies beyond the boundary of the Anatolian plateau. The official name of this inland region is the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Anatolia_Region" title="Eastern Anatolia Region">Eastern Anatolia Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Anatolians" title="Ancient Anatolians">ancient inhabitants of Anatolia</a> spoke the now-extinct <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a>, which were largely replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> starting from <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a> and during the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine</a> periods. Major Anatolian languages included <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Luwian_language" title="Luwian language">Luwian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lydian_language" title="Lydian language">Lydian</a> among other more poorly attested relatives. The <a href="/wiki/Turkification" title="Turkification">Turkification</a> of Anatolia began under the <a href="/wiki/Seljuk_Empire" title="Seljuk Empire">Seljuk Empire</a> in the late 11th century and continued under the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> between the late 13th and early 20th centuries. However, various non-Turkic languages continue to be spoken by minorities in Anatolia today, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_Neo-Aramaic" title="Assyrian Neo-Aramaic">Neo-Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenian_language" title="Armenian language">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laz_language" title="Laz language">Laz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgian_language" title="Georgian language">Georgian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>. Other ancient peoples in the region included <a href="/wiki/Galatians_(people)" title="Galatians (people)">Galatians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">Hurrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hattians" title="Hattians">Hattians</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cimmerian" title="Cimmerian">Cimmerians</a>, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_Greeks" title="Ionian Greeks">Ionian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dorian_Greeks" title="Dorian Greeks">Dorian</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aeolian_Greek" title="Aeolian Greek">Aeolian Greeks</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctext">Modern times</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Geology"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Geology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Climate"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Climate</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Ecoregions"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Ecoregions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Demographics"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Demographics</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Cuisine"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Cuisine</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Turkey" title="Geography of Turkey">Geography of Turkey</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="1158" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png/250px-Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png/375px-Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png/500px-Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Modern-day_Turkey_and_Europe_NASA_modified.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The location of <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> (within the rectangle) in reference to the <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European continent</a>. Anatolia roughly corresponds to the <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asian</a> part of Turkey</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:372px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2072" data-file-width="3170" decoding="async" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg/370px-1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg/555px-1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg/740px-1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg 2x" width="370"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1907_map_of_Asia_Minor-Atlas_of_Ancient_and_Classical_Geography_by_Samuel_Butler.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1907 map of Asia Minor, showing the local ancient kingdoms. The map includes the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Islands" title="Aegean Islands">East Aegean Islands</a> and the island of <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> to Anatolia\'s <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Traditionally, Anatolia is considered to extend in the east to an indefinite line running from the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Alexandretta" title="Gulf of Alexandretta">Gulf of Alexandretta</a> to the Black Sea,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niewohner2017_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Niewohner2017-9">[9]</a></sup> coterminous with the Anatolian Plateau. This traditional geographical definition is used, for example, in the latest edition of <i><a href="/wiki/Merriam-Webster%27s_Geographical_Dictionary" title="Merriam-Webster\'s Geographical Dictionary">Merriam-Webster\'s Geographical Dictionary</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merriam_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Merriam-1">[1]</a></sup> Under this definition, Anatolia is bounded to the east by the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Highlands" title="Armenian Highlands">Armenian Highlands</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> before that river bends to the southeast to enter <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-2">[2]</a></sup> To the southeast, it is bounded by the ranges that separate it from the Orontes valley in <a href="/wiki/Syria_(region)" title="Syria (region)">Syria (region)</a> and the Mesopotamian plain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mitchell_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mitchell-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide" title="Armenian genocide">Armenian genocide</a>, Ottoman Armenia <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Anatolia_Region#Substitution_with_Armenia" title="Eastern Anatolia Region">was renamed</a> "Eastern Anatolia" by the newly established Turkish government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sahakyan_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sahakyan-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Vazken Davidian terms the expanded use of "Anatolia" to apply to territory formerly referred to as Armenia an "ahistorical imposition", and notes that a growing body of literature is uncomfortable with referring to the Ottoman East as "Eastern Anatolia".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Most archeological sources consider the boundary of Anatolia to be Turkey\'s eastern border.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lori_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-lori-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The highest <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a> in Anatolia are <a href="/wiki/Mount_S%C3%BCphan" title="Mount S&#252;phan">Mount S&#252;phan</a> (4058 m) and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ararat" title="Mount Ararat">Mount Ararat</a> (5123 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Araxes" title="Araxes">Araxes</a> <a href="/wiki/Karasu_(Euphrates)" title="Karasu (Euphrates)">Karasu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Murat_river" title="Murat river">Murat rivers</a> connect the Anatolian plateau to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Caucasus" title="South Caucasus">South Caucasus</a> and the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Upper_Euphrates_Valley&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Upper Euphrates Valley (page does not exist)">Upper Euphrates Valley</a>. Along with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%87oruh" title="&#199;oruh">&#199;oruh</a>, these rivers are the longest in Anatolia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palumbi_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-palumbi-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The oldest known reference to Anatolia &#8211; as &#8220;Land of the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hatti</a>&#8221; &#8211; appears on <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a> <a href="/wiki/Cuneiform" title="Cuneiform">cuneiform</a> tablets from the period of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> (2350&#8211;2150 BC).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The first recorded name the Greeks used for the Anatolian peninsula, &#7944;&#963;&#943;&#945; (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Asia" title="wikt:Asia">As&#237;a</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> presumably echoed the name of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assuwa_league" title="Assuwa league">Assuwa league</a> in western Anatolia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> As the name "Asia" broadened its scope to apply to other areas east of the Mediterranean, Greeks in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> came to use the name &#924;&#953;&#954;&#961;&#8048; &#7944;&#963;&#943;&#945; (<i>Mikr&#225; As&#237;a</i>) or Asia Minor, meaning "Lesser Asia" to refer to present-day Anatolia.\n</p><p>The English-language name <i>Anatolia</i> itself derives from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Greek</a> <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#7936;&#957;&#945;&#964;&#959;&#955;&#942;</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">anatol&#7703;</i></i>) meaning &#8220;the East&#8221; or more literally &#8220;sunrise&#8221; (comparable to the Latin-derived terms "<a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">levant</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Orient" title="Orient">orient</a>").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-etym_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-etym-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The precise reference of this term has varied over time, perhaps originally referring to the <a href="/wiki/Aeolians" title="Aeolians">Aeolian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ionians" title="Ionians">Ionian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorians" title="Dorians">Dorian</a> colonies on the west coast of Asia Minor. In the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Anatolic_Theme" title="Anatolic Theme">Anatolic Theme</a> (&#7944;&#957;&#945;&#964;&#959;&#955;&#953;&#954;&#972;&#957; &#952;&#941;&#956;&#945;) was a <i><a href="/wiki/Theme_(Byzantine_district)" title="Theme (Byzantine district)">theme</a></i> covering the western and central parts of Turkey\'s present-day <a href="/wiki/Central_Anatolia_Region" title="Central Anatolia Region">Central Anatolia Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-First_Thema_1952,_p._59_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-First_Thema_1952,_p._59-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_Haldon_2002._Page_32_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_Haldon_2002._Page_32-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The term "Anatolia" is <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The modern Turkish form of Anatolia, <i>Anadolu</i>, derives from the Greek name A&#957;&#945;&#964;&#959;&#955;&#942; (<i>Anatol&#7703;</i>). The Russian male name <a href="/wiki/Anatoly" title="Anatoly">Anatoly</a> and the French <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Anatole_(disambiguation)" title="Anatole (disambiguation)">Anatole</a> share the same linguistic origin.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Geographical_name_changes_in_Turkey" title="Geographical name changes in Turkey">Geographical name changes in Turkey</a></div>\n<p>The term "Anatolia" originally referred to a northwestern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine" title="Byzantine">Byzantine</a> province. By the 12th century Europeans had started referring to Anatolia as <i>Turchia</i>. It has historically also been called "Asia Minor". In earlier times, it was called "(Land of the) R&#251;m" by both the Greeks and the <a href="/wiki/Seljuq_dynasty" title="Seljuq dynasty">Seljuqs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oxfordreference_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-oxfordreference-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the era of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> mapmakers outside the Empire referred to the mountainous plateau in eastern Anatolia as Armenia. Other contemporary sources called the same area <a href="/wiki/Kurdistan" title="Kurdistan">Kurdistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-suny_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-suny-23">[23]</a></sup> Geographers have variously used the terms east Anatolian plateau and Armenian plateau to refer to the region, although the territory encompassed by each term largely overlaps with the other. According to archaeologist <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lori_Khatchadourian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lori Khatchadourian (page does not exist)">Lori Khatchadourian</a> this difference in terminology "primarily result[s] from the shifting political fortunes and cultural trajectories of the region since the nineteenth century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lori_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-lori-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Turkey\'s <a href="/wiki/First_Geography_Congress,_Turkey" title="First Geography Congress, Turkey">First Geography Congress</a> in 1941 created two <a href="/wiki/Geographical_regions_of_Turkey" title="Geographical regions of Turkey">regions</a> to the east of the Gulf of Iskenderun-Black Sea line named the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Anatolia_Region" title="Eastern Anatolia Region">Eastern Anatolia Region</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Anatolia_Region" title="Southeastern Anatolia Region">Southeastern Anatolia Region</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yigit_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yigit-24">[24]</a></sup> the former largely corresponding to the western part of the Armenian Highland, the latter to the northern part of the Mesopotamian plain. According to  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Hovannisian" title="Richard Hovannisian">Richard Hovannisian</a> this changing of toponyms was "necessary to obscure all evidence" of Armenian presence as part of a campaign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armenian_genocide_denial" title="Armenian genocide denial">genocide denial</a> embarked upon by the newly established Turkish government and what Hovannisian calls its "foreign collaborators".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anatolia" title="History of Anatolia">History of Anatolia</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Prehistory_of_Anatolia" title="Prehistory of Anatolia">Prehistory of Anatolia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="807" data-file-width="1420" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg/300px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg/450px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg/600px-Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museum_of_Anatolian_Civilizations003.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mural of <a href="/wiki/Aurochs" title="Aurochs">aurochs</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Deer" title="Deer">deer</a>, and humans in <a href="/wiki/%C3%87atalh%C3%B6y%C3%BCk" title="&#199;atalh&#246;y&#252;k">&#199;atalh&#246;y&#252;k</a>, which is the largest and best-preserved Neolithic site found to date. It was registered as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> in 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Human habitation in Anatolia dates back to the <a href="/wiki/Paleolithic" title="Paleolithic">Paleolithic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> Anatolia has been <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_hypothesis" title="Anatolian hypothesis">proposed</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Urheimat" title="Urheimat">homeland</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European language family</a>, although linguists tend to favour a <a href="/wiki/Kurgan_hypothesis" title="Kurgan hypothesis">later origin</a> in the steppes north of the Black Sea. However, it is clear that the <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_languages" title="Anatolian languages">Anatolian languages</a>, the earliest attested branch of Indo-European, have been spoken in Anatolia since at least the 19th century BC.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Ancient_Near_East_.28Bronze_and_Iron_Ages.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_Near_East_(Bronze_and_Iron_Ages)">Ancient Near East (Bronze and Iron Ages)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Ancient Near East (Bronze and Iron Ages)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hattians_and_Hurrians">Hattians and Hurrians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hattians and Hurrians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The earliest historical records of Anatolia stem from the southeast of the region and are from the <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamian</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Sargon_of_Akkad" title="Sargon of Akkad">Sargon of Akkad</a> in the 24th century BC. Scholars generally believe the earliest indigenous populations of Anatolia were the <a href="/wiki/Hattians" title="Hattians">Hattians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurrians" title="Hurrians">Hurrians</a>. The Hattians spoke a <a href="/wiki/Hattic_language" title="Hattic language">language</a> of unclear affiliation, and the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian_language" title="Hurrian language">Hurrian language</a> belongs to a small family called <a href="/wiki/Hurro-Urartian_languages" title="Hurro-Urartian languages">Hurro-Urartian</a>, all these languages now being extinct; relationships with indigenous <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_the_Caucasus" title="Languages of the Caucasus">languages of the Caucasus</a> have been proposed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> but are not generally accepted. The region was famous for exporting raw materials, and areas of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hittite_empire" title="Hittite empire">Hattian</a>- and Hurrian-populated southeast Anatolia were colonised by the Akkadians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Assyrian_Empire_.2821st.E2.80.9318th_centuries_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyrian_Empire_(21st&#8211;18th_centuries_BC)">Assyrian Empire (21st&#8211;18th centuries BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Assyrian Empire (21st&#8211;18th centuries BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkadian Empire</a> in the mid-21st century BC, the Assyrians, who were the northern branch of the Akkadian people, colonised parts of the region between the 21st and mid-18th centuries BC and claimed its resources, notably <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>. One of the numerous cuneiform records dated circa 20th century BC, found in Anatolia at the Assyrian colony of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCltepe" title="K&#252;ltepe">Kanesh</a>, uses an advanced system of trading computations and credit lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Hittite_Kingdom_and_Empire_.2817th.E2.80.9312th_centuries_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Hittite_Kingdom_and_Empire_(17th&#8211;12th_centuries_BC)">Hittite Kingdom and Empire (17th&#8211;12th centuries BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hittite Kingdom and Empire (17th&#8211;12th centuries BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_Hittites" title="History of the Hittites">History of the Hittites</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lion_Gate,_Hattusa_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lion_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/220px-Lion_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lion_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/330px-Lion_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Lion_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg/440px-Lion_Gate%2C_Hattusa_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lion_Gate,_Hattusa_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Lion Gate at <a href="/wiki/Hattusa" title="Hattusa">Hattusa</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittite Empire</a>. The city\'s history dates to before 2000 BC.</div></div></div>\n<p>Unlike the Akkadians and their descendants, the Assyrians, whose Anatolian possessions were peripheral to their core lands in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Hittites" title="Hittites">Hittites</a> were centred at <a href="/wiki/Hattusa" title="Hattusa">Hattusa</a> (modern Bo&#287;azkale) in north-central Anatolia by the 17th century BC. They were speakers of an Indo-European language, the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite language</a>, or <i>nesili</i> (the language of Nesa) in Hittite. The Hittites originated of local ancient cultures that grew in Anatolia, in addition to the arrival of Indo-European languages. Attested for the first time in the Assyrian tablets of <a href="/wiki/K%C3%BCltepe" title="K&#252;ltepe">Nesa</a> around 2000BC, they conquered Hattusa in the 18th century BC, imposing themselves over Hattian- and Hurrian-speaking populations. According to the widely accepted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurgan_theory" title="Kurgan theory">Kurgan theory</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_homeland" title="Proto-Indo-European homeland">Proto-Indo-European homeland</a>, however, the Hittites (along with the other Indo-European <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Anatolians" title="Ancient Anatolians">ancient Anatolians</a>) were themselves relatively recent <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">immigrants</a> to Anatolia from the north. However, they did not necessarily displace the population genetically, they would rather assimilate into the former peoples\' culture, preserving the Hittite language however.\n</p><p>The Hittites adopted the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuneiform_script" title="Cuneiform script">cuneiform script</a>, invented in Mesopotamia. During the Late Bronze Age circa 1650 BC, they created a kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Hittites#New_Kingdom" title="Hittites">Hittite New Kingdom</a>, which became an empire in the 14th century BC after the conquest of <a href="/wiki/Kizzuwatna" title="Kizzuwatna">Kizzuwatna</a> in the south-east and the defeat of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assuwa_league" title="Assuwa league">Assuwa league</a> in western Anatolia. The empire reached its height in the 13th century BC, controlling much of Asia Minor, northwestern <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> and northwest upper Mesopotamia. They failed to reach the Anatolian coasts of the Black Sea, however, as a non-Indo-European people, the semi-nomadic pastoralist and tribal <a href="/wiki/Kaskians" title="Kaskians">Kaskians</a>, had established themselves there, displacing earlier <a href="/wiki/Palaic_language" title="Palaic language">Palaic-speaking</a> Indo-Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Much of the history of the Hittite Empire concerned war with the rival empires of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assyria" title="Assyria">Assyria</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mitanni" title="Mitanni">Mitanni</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roux_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roux-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> eventually withdrew from the region after failing to gain the upper hand over the Hittites and becoming wary of the power of Assyria, which had destroyed the Mitanni Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roux_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roux-31">[31]</a></sup> The Assyrians and Hittites were then left to battle over control of eastern and southern Anatolia and colonial territories in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>. The Assyrians had better success than the Egyptians, annexing much Hittite (and Hurrian) territory in these regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux_1966_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux_1966-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Neo-Hittite_kingdoms_.28c._1180.E2.80.93700_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Hittite_kingdoms_(c._1180&#8211;700_BC)">Neo-Hittite kingdoms (c. 1180&#8211;700 BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Neo-Hittite kingdoms (c. 1180&#8211;700 BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>After 1180 BC, during the <a href="/wiki/Late_Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Late Bronze Age collapse">Late Bronze Age collapse</a>, the Hittite empire disintegrated into several independent <a href="/wiki/Syro-Hittite_states" title="Syro-Hittite states">Syro-Hittite states</a>, subsequent to losing much territory to the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Assyrian_Empire" title="Middle Assyrian Empire">Middle Assyrian Empire</a> and being finally overrun by the <a href="/wiki/Phrygians" title="Phrygians">Phrygians</a>, another Indo-European people who are believed to have migrated from the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a>. The Phrygian expansion into southeast Anatolia was eventually halted by the Assyrians, who controlled that region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georges_Roux_1966_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Georges_Roux_1966-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><b>Arameans</b></dd></dl>\n<p><b><a href="/wiki/Arameans" title="Arameans">Arameans</a></b> encroached over the borders of south central Anatolia in the century or so after the fall of the Hittite empire, and some of the Syro-Hittite states in this region became an amalgam of Hittites and Arameans. These became known as <a href="/wiki/Syro-Hittite_states" title="Syro-Hittite states">Syro-Hittite states</a>.\n</p>\n<dl><dd><b>Luwians</b></dd></dl>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="541" data-file-width="896" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG/220px-Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG/330px-Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG/440px-Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dalyan_-_Caunos2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lycian rock cut tombs of <a href="/wiki/Kaunos" title="Kaunos">Kaunos</a> (Dalyan)</div></div></div>\n<p>In central and western Anatolia, another Indo-European people, the <a href="/wiki/Luwian_language" title="Luwian language">Luwians</a>, came to the fore, circa 2000 BC. Their language belonged to the same <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolic_branches&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Anatolic branches (page does not exist)">Anatolic clade</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Hittite_language" title="Hittite language">Hittite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The general consensus amongst scholars is that Luwian was spoken&#8212;to a greater or lesser degree&#8212;across a large area of western Anatolia, including (possibly) <a href="/wiki/Wilusa" title="Wilusa">Wilusa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>), the Seha River Land (to be identified with the <a href="/wiki/Gediz_River" title="Gediz River">Hermos</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Bak%C4%B1r%C3%A7ay" title="Bak&#305;r&#231;ay">Kaikos</a> valley), and the kingdom of Mira-Kuwaliya with its core territory of the Maeander valley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> From the 9th century BC, Luwian regions coalesced into a number of states such as <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a>, all of which had <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Hellenic</a> influence.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Neo-Assyrian_Empire_.2810th.E2.80.937th_centuries_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Assyrian_Empire_(10th&#8211;7th_centuries_BC)">Neo-Assyrian Empire (10th&#8211;7th centuries BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Neo-Assyrian Empire (10th&#8211;7th centuries BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From the 10th to late 7th centuries BC, much of Anatolia (particularly the southeastern regions) fell to the <a href="/wiki/Neo-Assyrian_Empire" title="Neo-Assyrian Empire">Neo-Assyrian Empire</a>, including all of the <a href="/wiki/Syro-Hittite_states" title="Syro-Hittite states">Syro-Hittite states</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tabal" title="Tabal">Tabal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Commagene" title="Kingdom of Commagene">Kingdom of Commagene</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> and swathes of <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>.\n</p><p>The Neo-Assyrian empire collapsed due to a bitter series of civil wars followed by a combined attack by <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, Scythians and their own <a href="/wiki/Babylonia" title="Babylonia">Babylonian</a> relations. The last Assyrian city to fall was <a href="/wiki/Harran" title="Harran">Harran</a> in southeast Anatolia. This city was the birthplace of the last king of <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, the Assyrian <a href="/wiki/Nabonidus" title="Nabonidus">Nabonidus</a> and his son and regent <a href="/wiki/Belshazzar" title="Belshazzar">Belshazzar</a>. Much of the region then fell to the short-lived Iran-based <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Median Empire</a>, with the Babylonians and Scythians briefly appropriating some territory.\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Cimmerian_and_Scythian_invasions_.288th.E2.80.937th_centuries_BC.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Cimmerian_and_Scythian_invasions_(8th&#8211;7th_centuries_BC)">Cimmerian and Scythian invasions (8th&#8211;7th centuries BC)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Cimmerian and Scythian invasions (8th&#8211;7th centuries BC)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>From the late 8th century BC, a new wave of Indo-European-speaking raiders entered northern and northeast Anatolia: the <a href="/wiki/Cimmerians" title="Cimmerians">Cimmerians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a>. The Cimmerians overran <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a> and the Scythians threatened to do the same to Urartu and <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a>, before both were finally checked by the Assyrians.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_West">Greek West</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Greek West">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="405" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg/170px-Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg/255px-Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg/340px-Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Achaemenid_Satrap_Asia_Minor_end_of_6th_century_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> Satrap of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> (<a href="/wiki/Heraclea_Pontica" title="Heraclea Pontica">Heraclea</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>), end of 6th century BCE, probably under Darius I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The north-western coast of Anatolia was inhabited by Greeks of the <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(tribe)" title="Achaeans (tribe)">Achaean</a>/<a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean</a> culture from the 20th century BC, related to the Greeks of south eastern Europe and the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Islands" title="Aegean Islands">Aegean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">[36]</a></sup> Beginning with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronze_Age_collapse" title="Bronze Age collapse">Bronze Age collapse</a> at the end of the 2nd millennium BC, the west coast of Anatolia was settled by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ionian_Greeks" title="Ionian Greeks">Ionian Greeks</a>, usurping the area of the related but earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greeks" title="Mycenaean Greeks">Mycenaean Greeks</a>. Over several centuries, numerous Ancient Greek <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a> were established on the coasts of Anatolia. Greeks started Western philosophy on the western coast of Anatolia (<a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">Pre-Socratic philosophy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Antiquity">Classical Antiquity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Classical Antiquity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4027" data-file-width="6167" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg/300px-Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg/450px-Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg/600px-Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_in_the_Greco-Roman_period_-_general_map_-_regions_and_main_settlements.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asia Minor in the Greco-Roman period. The classical regions and their main settlements</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4027" data-file-width="6167" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg/300px-Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg/450px-Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg/600px-Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_in_the_2nd_century_AD_-_general_map_-_Roman_provinces_under_Trajan_-_bleached_-_English_legend.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asia Minor in the early 2nd century AD. The Roman provinces under Trajan.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg/220px-Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg/330px-Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg/440px-Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Templeofathenaprienemay2007.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The temple of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> (funded by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In classical antiquity, Anatolia was described by <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and later historians as divided into regions that were diverse in culture, language and religious practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yavuz_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-yavuz-37">[37]</a></sup> The northern regions included <a href="/wiki/Bithynia" title="Bithynia">Bithynia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paphlagonia" title="Paphlagonia">Paphlagonia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a>; to the west were <a href="/wiki/Mysia" title="Mysia">Mysia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lydia" title="Lydia">Lydia</a> and Caria; and <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> belonged to the southern shore. There were also several inland regions: <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cappadocia" title="Cappadocia">Cappadocia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pisidia" title="Pisidia">Pisidia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yavuz_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-yavuz-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dying_gaul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1186" data-file-width="1458" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Dying_gaul.jpg/220px-Dying_gaul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Dying_gaul.jpg/330px-Dying_gaul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Dying_gaul.jpg/440px-Dying_gaul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dying_gaul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Dying_Gaul" title="Dying Gaul">Dying Galatian</a></i> was a famous statue commissioned some time between <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">230&#8211;220 BC</a> by King <a href="/wiki/Attalus_I" title="Attalus I">Attalos I</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Pergamon</a> to honor his victory over the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> <a href="/wiki/Galatia" title="Galatia">Galatians</a> in Anatolia.</div></div></div>\n<p>Anatolia is known as the birthplace of minted <a href="/wiki/Coin" title="Coin">coinage</a> (as opposed to unminted coinage, which first appears in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> at a much earlier date) as a medium of exchange, some time in the 7th century BC in Lydia. The use of minted coins continued to flourish during the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> eras.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 6th century BC, all of Anatolia was conquered by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persian</a> <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, the Persians having usurped the <a href="/wiki/Medes" title="Medes">Medes</a> as the dominant dynasty in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>. In 499 BC, the Ionian city-states on the west coast of Anatolia rebelled against Persian rule. The <a href="/wiki/Ionian_Revolt" title="Ionian Revolt">Ionian Revolt</a>, as it became known, though quelled, initiated the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>, which ended in a Greek victory in 449 BC, and the Ionian cities regained their independence, alongside the withdrawal of the Persian forces from their European territories.\n</p><p>In 334 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian</a> Greek king <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> conquered the peninsula from the Achaemenid Persian Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Alexander\'s conquest opened up the interior of Asia Minor to Greek settlement and influence.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3302" data-file-width="4953" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg/220px-Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg/330px-Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg/440px-Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nemrut_Da%C4%9F%C4%B1_12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sanctuary of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commagene" title="Commagene">Commagene</a> Kings on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Nemrut" title="Mount Nemrut">Mount Nemrut</a> (1st century BC)</div></div></div>\n<p>Following the death of Alexander and the breakup of his empire, Anatolia was ruled by a series of Hellenistic kingdoms, such as the <a href="/wiki/Attalid_dynasty" title="Attalid dynasty">Attalids of Pergamum</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seleucids" title="Seleucids">Seleucids</a>, the latter controlling most of Anatolia. A period of peaceful <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a> followed, such that the local Anatolian languages had been supplanted by Greek by the 1st century BC. In 133 BC the last Attalid king bequeathed his kingdom to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>, and western and central Anatolia came under <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman</a> control, but <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_culture" title="Hellenistic culture">Hellenistic culture</a> remained predominant. Further annexations by Rome, in particular of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Pontus" title="Kingdom of Pontus">Kingdom of Pontus</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>, brought all of Anatolia under Roman control, except for the eastern frontier with the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a>, which remained unstable for centuries, causing a series of wars, culminating in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman-Parthian_Wars" title="Roman-Parthian Wars">Roman-Parthian Wars</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Christian_Period">Early Christian Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Early Christian Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>After the division of the Roman Empire, Anatolia became part of the East Roman, or <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Anatolia was one of the first places where Christianity spread, so that by the 4th century AD, western and central Anatolia were overwhelmingly Christian and Greek-speaking. For the next 600 years, while Imperial possessions in Europe were subjected to barbarian invasions, Anatolia would be the center of the Hellenic world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>It was one of the wealthiest and most densely populated places in the Late <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Anatolia\'s wealth grew during the 4th and 5th centuries thanks, in part, to the <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim%27s_Road" title="Pilgrim\'s Road">Pilgrim\'s Road</a> that ran through the peninsula. Literary evidence about the rural landscape has come down to us from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hagiographies" title="Hagiographies">hagiographies</a> of 6th century <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_of_Sion" title="Nicholas of Sion">Nicholas of Sion</a> and 7th century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Sykeon" title="Theodore of Sykeon">Theodore of Sykeon</a>. Large urban centers included <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pergamum" title="Pergamum">Pergamum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aphrodisias" title="Aphrodisias">Aphrodisias</a>. Scholars continue to debate the cause of urban decline in the 6th and 7th centuries variously attributing it to the <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a> (541), and the 7th century <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_War_of_602%E2%80%93628" title="Byzantine&#8211;Sasanian War of 602&#8211;628">Persian incursion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab_conquest_of_the_Levant" title="Arab conquest of the Levant">Arab conquest of the Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thonemann_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-thonemann-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the ninth and tenth century a resurgent Byzantine Empire regained its lost territories, including even long lost territory such as <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a> (ancient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aram_(biblical_region)" title="Aram (biblical region)">Aram</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Medieval_Period">Late Medieval Period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Late Medieval Period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="687" data-file-width="1317" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg/300px-Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg/450px-Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg/600px-Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_Minor_ca_842_AD.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Anatolia" title="Byzantine Anatolia">Byzantine Anatolia</a> and the Byzantine-Arab frontier zone in the mid-9th century</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anatolia1300.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="535" decoding="async" height="185" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Anatolia1300.png/300px-Anatolia1300.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Anatolia1300.png/450px-Anatolia1300.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Anatolia1300.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anatolia1300.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Beyliks and other states around Anatolia, c. 1300.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 10 years following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Manzikert" title="Battle of Manzikert">Battle of Manzikert</a> in 1071, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seljuk_Turks" title="Seljuk Turks">Seljuk Turks</a> from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> migrated over large areas of Anatolia, with particular concentrations around the northwestern rim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The Turkish language and the Islamic religion were gradually introduced as a result of the Seljuk conquest, and this period marks the start of Anatolia\'s slow transition from predominantly Christian and Greek-speaking, to predominantly Muslim and Turkish-speaking (although ethnic groups such as Armenians, Greeks, and Assyrians remained numerous and retained Christianity and their native languages). In the following century, the Byzantines managed to reassert their control in western and northern Anatolia. Control of Anatolia was then split between the Byzantine Empire and the Seljuk <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sultanate_of_R%C3%BBm" title="Sultanate of R&#251;m">Sultanate of R&#251;m</a>, with the Byzantine holdings gradually being reduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balyuzi_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balyuzi-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1255, the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongols</a> swept through eastern and central Anatolia, and would remain until 1335. The <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> garrison was stationed near <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balyuzi_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Balyuzi-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> After the decline of the Ilkhanate from 1335&#8211;1353, the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>\'s legacy in the region was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Uyghur_people" title="Uyghur people">Uyghur</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eretna_Dynasty" title="Eretna Dynasty">Eretna Dynasty</a> that was overthrown by <a href="/wiki/Kadi_Burhan_al-Din" title="Kadi Burhan al-Din">Kadi Burhan al-Din</a> in 1381.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of the 14th century, most of Anatolia was controlled by various <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_beyliks" title="Anatolian beyliks">Anatolian beyliks</a>. Smyrna fell in 1330, and the last Byzantine stronghold in Anatolia, Philadelphia, fell in 1390. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turkmen_people" title="Turkmen people">Turkmen</a> Beyliks were under the control of the Mongols, at least nominally, through declining Seljuk sultans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The Beyliks did not mint coins in the names of their own leaders while they remained under the suzerainty of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilkhanids" title="Ilkhanids">Ilkhanids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Osmanli" title="Osmanli">Osmanli</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Osman_I" title="Osman I">Osman I</a> was the first Turkish ruler who minted coins in his own name in 1320s, for it bears the legend "Minted by Osman son of Ertugrul".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Since the minting of coins was a prerogative accorded in Islamic practice only to a <a href="/wiki/Sovereignty" title="Sovereignty">sovereign</a>, it can be considered that the Osmanli, or Ottoman Turks, became formally independent from the Mongol Khans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Empire">Ottoman Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ottoman Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turkish</a> leaders, the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_dynasty" title="Ottoman dynasty">Ottomans</a> emerged as great power under <a href="/wiki/Osman_I" title="Osman I">Osman I</a> and his son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orhan_I" title="Orhan I">Orhan I</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The Anatolian <a href="/wiki/Anatolian_beyliks" title="Anatolian beyliks">beyliks</a> were successively absorbed into the rising <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> during the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> It is not well understood how the Osmanl&#305;, or <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Turks</a>, came to dominate their neighbours, as the history of medieval Anatolia is still little known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The Ottomans completed the conquest of the peninsula in 1517 with the taking of <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">Halicarnassus</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Bodrum" title="Bodrum">Bodrum</a>) from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knights_of_Saint_John" title="Knights of Saint John">Knights of Saint John</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_times">Modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ethnicturkey1911.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="818" data-file-width="1144" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ethnicturkey1911.jpg/300px-Ethnicturkey1911.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ethnicturkey1911.jpg/450px-Ethnicturkey1911.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Ethnicturkey1911.jpg/600px-Ethnicturkey1911.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ethnicturkey1911.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ethnographic map of Anatolia from 1911.</div></div></div>\n<p>With the acceleration of the decline of the Ottoman Empire in the early 19th century, and as a result of the expansionist policies of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, many Muslim nations and groups in that region, mainly <a href="/wiki/Circassians" title="Circassians">Circassians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatars" title="Tatars">Tatars</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Azeris" title="Azeris">Azeris</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lezgian_people" title="Lezgian people">Lezgis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chechens" title="Chechens">Chechens</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> groups left their homelands and settled in Anatolia. As the Ottoman Empire further shrank in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> regions and then fragmented during the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Wars" title="Balkan Wars">Balkan Wars</a>, much of the non-Christian populations of its former possessions, mainly Balkan Muslims (Bosnian Muslims, <a href="/wiki/Albanians" title="Albanians">Albanians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_people" title="Turkish people">Turks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomaks" title="Pomaks">Muslim Bulgarians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Muslims" title="Greek Muslims">Greek Muslims</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Vallahades" title="Vallahades">Vallahades</a> from <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(Greece)" title="Macedonia (Greece)">Greek Macedonia</a>), were resettled in various parts of Anatolia, mostly in formerly Christian villages throughout Anatolia.\n</p><p>A continuous reverse migration occurred since the early 19th century, when Greeks from Anatolia, <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a> and Pontus area migrated toward the newly independent Kingdom of Greece, and also towards the United States, southern part of the Russian Empire, Latin America and rest of Europe.\n</p><p>Following the Russo-Persian Treaty of Turkmenchay (1828) and the incorporation of the Eastern Armenia into the Russian Empire, another migration involved the large Armenian population of Anatolia, which recorded significant migration rates from Western Armenia (Eastern Anatolia) toward the Russian Empire, especially toward its newly established Armenian provinces.\n</p><p>Anatolia remained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multi-ethnic" title="Multi-ethnic">multi-ethnic</a> until the early 20th century (see the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rise_of_nationalism_under_the_Ottoman_Empire" title="Rise of nationalism under the Ottoman Empire">rise of nationalism under the Ottoman Empire</a>). During World War I, the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Genocide" title="Armenian Genocide">Armenian Genocide</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a> (especially in <a href="/wiki/Pontus_(region)" title="Pontus (region)">Pontus</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_genocide" title="Assyrian genocide">Assyrian genocide</a> almost entirely removed the ancient indigenous communities of <a href="/wiki/Armenians" title="Armenians">Armenian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Assyrian_people" title="Assyrian people">Assyrian</a> populations in Anatolia and surrounding regions. Following the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_of_1919%E2%80%931922" title="Greco-Turkish War of 1919&#8211;1922">Greco-Turkish War of 1919&#8211;1922</a>, most remaining ethnic Anatolian Greeks were forced out during the 1923 <a href="/wiki/Population_exchange_between_Greece_and_Turkey" title="Population exchange between Greece and Turkey">population exchange between Greece and Turkey</a>. Many more have left Turkey since, leaving fewer than 5,000 Greeks in Anatolia today. Since the foundation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Turkey" title="Republic of Turkey">Republic of Turkey</a> in 1923, Anatolia has been within Turkey, its inhabitants being mainly Turks and Kurds (see <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Turkey" title="Demographics of Turkey">demographics of Turkey</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Turkey" title="History of Turkey">history of Turkey</a>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geology_of_Turkey" title="Geology of Turkey">Geology of Turkey</a></div>\n<p>Anatolia\'s terrain is structurally complex. A central <a href="/wiki/Massif" title="Massif">massif</a> composed of uplifted blocks and downfolded <a href="/wiki/Trough_(geology)" title="Trough (geology)">troughs</a>, covered by recent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deposit_(geology)" title="Deposit (geology)">deposits</a> and giving the appearance of a plateau with rough terrain, is wedged between two folded mountain ranges that converge in the east. True lowland is confined to a few narrow coastal strips along the Aegean, Mediterranean, and Black Sea coasts. Flat or gently sloping land is rare and largely confined to the deltas of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%B1z%C4%B1l_River" title="K&#305;z&#305;l River">K&#305;z&#305;l River</a>, the coastal plains of <a href="/wiki/%C3%87ukurova" title="&#199;ukurova">&#199;ukurova</a> and the valley floors of the <a href="/wiki/Gediz_River" title="Gediz River">Gediz River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/B%C3%BCy%C3%BCk_Menderes_River" title="B&#252;y&#252;k Menderes River">B&#252;y&#252;k Menderes River</a> as well as some interior high plains in Anatolia, mainly around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Tuz" title="Lake Tuz">Lake Tuz</a> (Salt Lake) and the <a href="/wiki/Konya" title="Konya">Konya</a> Basin (<i>Konya Ovasi</i>).\n</p><p>There are two mountain ranges in southern Anatolia: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taurus_mountains" title="Taurus mountains">Taurus</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zagros" title="Zagros">Zagros</a> mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wiley_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-wiley-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Turkey" title="Climate of Turkey">Climate of Turkey</a></div>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional" style="max-width: 489px;_width: 489px;">\n<li class="gallerycaption">Temperatures of Anatolia</li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:33.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Klima_ankara.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Klima_ankara.png/120px-Klima_ankara.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Klima_ankara.png/180px-Klima_ankara.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Klima_ankara.png/240px-Klima_ankara.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">Ankara</a> (central Anatolia)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:33.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Klima_antalya.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Klima_antalya.png/120px-Klima_antalya.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Klima_antalya.png/180px-Klima_antalya.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Klima_antalya.png/240px-Klima_antalya.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Antalya" title="Antalya">Antalya</a> (southern Anatolia)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px"><div style="width: 155px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;"><div style="margin:33.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Klima_van.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Klima_van.png/120px-Klima_van.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Klima_van.png/180px-Klima_van.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Klima_van.png/240px-Klima_van.png 2x" width="120"/></a></div></div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Van,_Turkey" title="Van, Turkey">Van</a> (eastern Anatolia)\n</p>\n</div>\n</div></li>\n</ul>\n<p>Anatolia has a varied range of climates. The central plateau is characterized by a continental climate, with hot summers and cold snowy winters. The south and west coasts enjoy a typical Mediterranean climate, with mild rainy winters, and warm dry summers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prothero19_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prothero19-57">[57]</a></sup> The Black Sea and Marmara coasts have a temperate oceanic climate, with cool foggy summers and much rainfall throughout the year.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecoregions">Ecoregions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Anatolia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ecoregions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There is a diverse number of plant and animal communities.\n</p><p>The mountains and coastal plain of northern Anatolia experiences humid and mild climate. There are <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">temperate broadleaf, mixed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temperate_coniferous_forest" title="Temperate coniferous forest">coniferous</a> forests. The central and eastern plateau, with its drier <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climate</a>, has deciduous forests and forest steppes.
